Blistering cups are not applied except on members where one cannot put blistering cups because of their smallness, such as lips and gums, and similar places; and on fleshless places, such as elbows or knees, and others similar; and the quality of the water is that when you find blisters that are in sweet clean water with porridge, take them; and put one in sweet clean water, until they are famished and have nothing in their bellies; and then be bled from vein and blistering cup, then rub the member as much as it is red, and put on the blisters; and when they are full and swollen, if it is a place where one can put a blistering cup, then put them, for this is worth much afterwards.
Se ceu non, si leve le leu d’aisil, et puis d’aigue, mout bien, et puis l’espreint; et se li leus saine trop aprés lou cors des sansues, si moille I drapel de lin en aigue froide, et met sus, tant que li sans soit estenchiés; et se li sans est multipliez, si met sus vitriole broié, ou gales, et semblans choses, tant qu’il soit estenchiez; ou tu met sor le leu moitiez de feves pelees, et les laisse tant qu’elles s’aerdent au leu, si estanchera maintenant; et se tu en as de plus mestier, si pren autres sansues.	If not, then raise the shoulder joint with a lever made of ash and then an iron spike, firmly, and then it is pierced; and if the joint swells too much after the bleeding, moisten the linen cloth in cold water, put it on, until the swelling subsides; and if there are many swellings, put on vitriol paste or galls, and similar things, until they subside; or you place half of split peas on the joint and let them heat up against the joint, so it will now stop bleeding; and if you need more, take other swabbing.
Et se les sansues ne se vuellent prendre, si frote le leu de sanc fres, ou tu i fiche une aguille tant que li sans en isse, et puis les met sus, et tantost com elles sentent lou sanc, si s’aerdent; et quant tu vues qu’elles chieent, si poudre sus un poc d’aloen et de seil broiet, ou de cendre, si cherront maintenant.	And if the swellings do not want to take hold, then rub the fresh blood on the joint, or you stick in a needle until the swelling comes out, and then put them on, and as soon as they feel the blood, they will heat up; and when you see that they are seeping, sprinkle some aloes and burnt linen dust, or ashes, so they will now stop.
Ci comence li livre des brisures	Here begins the book of bruises.
Cist chapistres est maintes fois necessaires en l’art de medicine, et est restorement de brisure et de desaluement.	This chapter is many times necessary in the art of medicine, and it serves as a remedy for bruising and inflammation.
Saichiez, chier fil, que li fol mire se gietent de cest chapistre, qu’il ne virent onques nul des anciens livres, ne lettre n’en lurent; et por ceste cause est estraingie li science en nostre terre, car je n’en trovai onques nul boin; et n’en sai nient, fuer que par la science des anciens livres, ou j’ai tant estudiiet que je en ai traite la science, et par experiment, et par l’acostumance de ma vie; et je vos ai jai descrit en cest chapistre ceu qui est contenut, et l’ai abregié en la fin, et ai mostreit clerement les formes des estrumens que sont amenistrez, si com j’ai fait en ces II chapistres ci devant.	Listen, dear son, that many a fool wonders at this chapter, who have never seen any of the ancient books or letter about it; and because of this reason is the science unusual in our land, for I never found good one; and know nothing about it, except through the science of the ancient books, which I have studied so much that I have dealt with its science, and by experiment, and by the custom of my life; and I have described to you in this chapter what is contained, and abbreviated at the end, and clearly shown the forms of the instruments that are brought, just as I have done in these two chapters before.
Il covient donques que nos disons ou prologue de cest livre les sommes de la parole et les chapistres ou li saiges met son entendement, et demore sus la verteit, si que cil qui sont esproveit saichent et aprenent cest art noble, et l’aprenent a autrui.	Therefore it is necessary that we say the prologue of this book, the summaries of the words and the chapters where the wise man puts his understanding, and remains on the truth, so that those who have been proven know and learn this noble art, and teach it to others.
Je dis que quant brisure ou dislocation ou torsions ou quasseure vient a aucun, si covient que tu te hastes de sainier lou malaide d’une part et d’autre, s’aucune chose ne lou te tot, si com foiblece de cors, ou enfes, ou viellars; ou se li tens n’est tres chaus, ou trop frois; et menjuce cholz frois, c’est a savoir froides joutes, et char d’oiseil et de chevril, et ne boive point de vin, ne menjuce nules grasses chars, et ne s’emplisse mie trop de viandes qui remplissent les voinnes de sanc, tant que tu n’aies doute d’aposteme chaut, ne d’espandement de matiere au membre; si reviegne li malaides a som premerain governement k’il a useit.	I say that when breakage or dislocation or twisting or cracking comes to anyone, it is necessary that you hasten to heal the sick person on both sides, without any other thing, such as weakness of the body, or infirmity, or old age; or if the time is not very hot, or too cold; and do not give him cold choleric meat, that is to say, cold broth, and meat of chicken and goat, and do not let him drink wine, nor give him any fatty meats, and do not fill his veins with blood so full that you have doubt of warm abscess, or spreading of matter in the member; if the sick person recovers at first by your previous care.
Et quant li os brisiés comence a restourer, si covient que on norrisse le malaide de viandes que font gros norrissement, ou il ait viscositeit, si com froment cuit en aigue, et ris, et testes, et piez, et pance de vaiche, et oefz, et poissons fres, et gros vin, et semblans chose.	And when the broken bone begins to heal, it is necessary that you nourish the sick person with foods that make thick nourishment, or have viscosity, such as bread cooked in water, and rice, and cheese, and meat pies, and veal pâté, and eggs, and fresh fish, and strong wine, and similar things.
Car a teil norrissement se reprent mues et plus tost li brisure.	For with good nourishment the swelling goes away and the bruise heals faster.
Et saichiez que li os brisiez des vielz et des decrepites ne se pueent mie bien reprendre ne souder selonc lor nature por la sechor des membres, ne cil ausi qui sont trop tendre, si com os de petis enfans; mais n’a cure, ramoinne sus l’os brisiet I gros tenrons qui s’aert et joint le membre ensamble, tant qu’il enforce si com il fut devant; et ne li tot point de son oevre, et por ceu le covient norrir de grosses viandes, si com nos avons dit.	And know that the broken bones of the old and decrepit cannot be well reunited or soldered according to their nature due to the drying out of the members, nor also those which are too tender, such as the bones of small children; but do not care, reset on the fractured bone the large tendons that are firm and unite the member together, so long as it strengthens itself as before; and leave him completely without further work, and because of this he must be nourished with thick meats, as we have said.
Et saiches que les briseures sont diverses em maintes manieres.	And know that bruises are diverse in many ways.
Car li brisure dou chief est autre que li brisure dou pis, ou de la jambe, et je vos dirai en cest chapistre toutes manieres de brisure.	For the bruise of the head is different from the bruise of the belly or leg, and I will tell you in this chapter all the ways of bruises.
Car il i a brisure que vait a arvol, et autre en aguisant, si com li taille d’une penne por escrire.	For there are bruises that turn into abscesses, and others that become inflamed, such as the cut of a quill for writing.
Et si a brisure avec plaie, et brisure a tout une petite fendure.	And there is a bruise with an open wound, and a bruise with a small crack or fissure throughout it.
Et a chescune maniere ait son engien, et son estrument propre en la restauration, selonc ceu que li remembrance vient en son leu; et est conue li brisure de l’os par ceu que li leus est tors, et apert bien par defuer, et sone au presseir de la main; et quant il n’i a tortuositeit ne son au tochier, et li malaides ne sent mie grant dolor, se n’i a point de brisure; mais il puet bien avoir tortuositeit, ou fendure, ou petite brisure.	And at each manner he should have his device, and proper instrument in the restoration, according to that which brings the remembrance back into its place; and it is known that the fracture of the bone by that which the joint is twisted, and it opens well apart, and sounds with the pressure of the hand; and when there is no twisting or sound when touched, and the patient does not feel great pain, then there is no break; but it can still have a twist, or fissure, or small break.
Si ne covient mie que tu lou mueves a la main, mais met sus medicines que vienent ci aprés, et puis l’estraint maintenant subtilment.	So do not move the member with your hand, but put on medicines that come later, and then bind it now gently.
Et saiches que li brisure que vait en arvol, et sont les II parties desjointes, il les covient maintenant mettre a point igalment, ainsois que apostemes chaus i viegne; et se apostemes chaulz i vient, si lou laisse aucun jor, tant que li apostemes soit apaisiez; puis le met igal au mues que tu pues legierement et saigement, et saiches que li restauration de li est plus legiere que li restaurations de celi ou il ait pieces; puis l’estraint, si com il covient ci aprés.	And know that the fracture that goes in a circle, and are the two parts separated, you must now put them to equal points, thus as abscesses will form; and if abscesses form, let him be left alone for a day, until the abscess is appeased; then put it on an axis so that you can move lightly and easily; and know that the restoration of the arm is more easier than the restorations of those where there are pieces; then bind it, as it needs to be done afterwards.
Et se en l’os a piéces brisies, si l’estent de II pars, se c’est mains ou piet, ou a ta main se c’est uns petis membres, ou a II cordes, ou a une corde et a ta main; et met le membre selonc sa figure natureil, si que quant les parties dou membre brisiet sont desevrees, si ramoinne par toz engiens les additions en lor leus au mues que tu pues, et garde que par ton ovraige ne viegne au malaide lesion ne doulor; et ajoste bien les II chiés des os selonc les millors formes.	And if in the bone there are broken pieces, if it is a hand or foot, or on your hand it is a small limb, or two strings, or one string and to your hand; and put the member according to its natural shape, so that when the parts of the broken member have been separated, then reset by all means the added pieces in their places at the joints as you can, and be careful that through your work does not come to the patient injury or pain; and join well the two ends of the bones according to the best forms.
Et le sent bien a la main s’il te semble k’il ne soit mie bien: si la dresce bellement, et ne l’estraindre mie trop, ne presser si com font cil qui i font venir par lor ovraige aposteme chaut et contraction au membre, si com nos avons veut maintes fois.	And if it feels right to your hand that it is not well, then straighten it well, and do not bind it too tightly, nor press it as those who by their work bring hot apostemes and contractions to the member, as we have said many times.
Et quant li membres est a point, si chastie bien lou malaide qu’il ne lou mueve, ne en veillant ne en dormant, ne en tornant, ne en autre maniere; et warde bien que li membres soit si asseurez qu’il ne sente nule dolor.	And when the member is in place, then punish the patient well so that it does not move, neither while watching nor sleeping, nor turning, nor in any other way; and beware that the member be so secure that it feels no pain.
Car quant il sent nulle dolor, si n’est mie a point, et le covient mettre en teil disposition qu’il ne sent nule dolor; et te garde k’il soit mis droitement, que tortuositez n’i veigne quant il iert restorez; et li qualitez dou membre brisiet est selonc ceu que je te dis.	For when it feels no pain, then it is not in place, and you must put it in that disposition so that it feels no pain; and take care that it be set straightly, that no crookedness come into it when it is restored; and the quality of the broken member will be according to what I am telling you.
Saiches que li membre brisiet sont divers em petitece et en grandor et en forme.	Know that broken members are diverse in smallness and greatness and form.
Car il i a petis membres, si com brais et dois et semblans: si covient envoloupeir drapelz de lin molz et subtilz; et cil qui sont gros, si com cuixe et jambe: si covient envoloupeir d’un liien large et dur, car li larges liiens embraice tout le grant membre, et l’estraint de toutes pars igalment, si qu’il n’i a nule taiche; puis lie entor estoupes legieres, III tours ou IIII selonc lou membre, et estraint ta main un poc a tout le liien, puis vai au desus de la brisure et l’estraint moins, que la brisure puis esloigne un poc la brisure; et mollifie lou liien poc et poc, tant que tu preignes grant partie dou leu sain, puis repren I autre liien, et torne entor derechief sus lou leu brisiet; puis revai a la partie desoz de la brisure, et l’estraint moienement, ne mie trop fort ne trop molement, si com nos avons dit en la premiere lieure desus; puis met entre les liiens estoupes de lin ou drapelz, tant qu’il soit toz igalz, puis lie tout par desus I bendeil, et puis met tes esteles bien faites sus, s’il n’i a aposteme ou enfleure.	For there are small members, such as arms and legs and similar things: you must envelop them in a cloth of soft and subtle linen; and those that are large, such as calves and thighs: you must envelop them in a bandage of wide and hard string, for the broad strings encircle all the great member and hold it even from all sides, so that there is no swelling; then tie around light compresses, three turns or four according to the member, and strain your hand on a peg against all the string, then go up above the break and hold it less tightly, so that the break will be separated by a little; and soften the string gradually, as much as you take firm part of the healthy place; then take the other bandage and turn around again upwards upon the broken place; then return to the lower part under the break and hold it moderately, not too tight nor too gently, such as we have said in the first lesson above; then put between the bandages compresses of linen or cloth, so long as they are all even, then tie everything together with a strip, and then put your splints well made on them, if there is no abscess or swelling.
Et s’il i a enfleure ou aposteme, si met sus chose apaisant aposteme chaut, et qui oste l’enfleure, et le laisse ensi aucuns jors; puis estrain sus tes esteles, et soient de moitiet d’une large cainne rese saigement, ou de fust dont on fait les clives, ou de rains de palme, et soient selonc ceste figure:	If there is an inflammation or abscess, place a soothing paste on it that is hot and removes the inflammation, and let it stay for some days; then bind on some stars, which are half of a wide awl sharpened, or of wood from which one makes splints, or of palm rushes, and be according to this figure:
mais il covient que li estele que om met sus la brisure soit un poc plus grosse et plus large que les autres; et soit ausi longe com il covient au membre de petitece ou de grandor; puis estraint sus les esteles ausi com tu as fait a la premiere lieure, puis lie desus un fil avenanment, si com en la premiere lieure sus la briseure; plus estroit est ensus, adés moins; et covient que li fil soient moien, ne trop gros ne trop menus, et soient de lin mol; car se li fil sont gros, si com j’ai veut des oevres des folz qui font lor lieures des cordes de linement tort, et c’est grant error, car li lieure ne puet estre igal s’il est trop subtilz, et ne covient mie qu’il ait entre chescune estele moins dou doi d’espaice.	But it is necessary that the star that you put on the break is a little thicker and wider than the others; and be as long as is needed for the member of small or great size; then bind above the stars in the same way as you did at the first touch, then tie above with a cord smoothly, as was done at the first touch on the break; the more narrow it is above, the better; and it must be that the cord is medium, not too thick nor too thin, and they are of fine linen; for if the cord is thick, like I have seen in the works of fools who make their bandages from twisted liniment threads, and this is a great error, because the bandage cannot be equal if it is too subtle, and it must not have between each star less than two fingers' breadth.
Et se li malaides est navrez par les chiés des esteles aprés l’estrignement en leus sains, si met desoz elles estoupes de lin, ou lainne charpie, si qu’il ne se blece.	And if the malady is injured by the sharpness of the threads after the straining in its healthy parts, then he places under them poultices of linen or rough wool, so that it does not bleed.
Quant li plaie est avec la briseure, et li keurs est rompus, je en dirai la cure ci aprés.	When the wound is with the breakage and the stitches are broken, I will say the treatment afterwards.
Et saiches qu’il ne covient mie estraindre d’esteles toz les membres brisiez le premier jor.	And know that it's not necessary to strain stars on all broken limbs in the first day.
Car quant li membres est grans, se n’i covient mie mettre esteles fuer que aprés V jors ou VII, ou plus selonc ta seurteit d’aposteme chaut sorvenant; et vez ci la maniere des emplaustres anciiens por restorer brisure, dislocation, et torsion: et est que tu pren de la farine que se tient as parois dou molin, et la confis avec abun d’uef, et met ceste confection, ne trop clere ne trop espesse, et en use, et c’est li commune maniere de l’emplaustre restorant la brisure a plusors dispositions, et meismement as femmes et as petis enfans, car il n’est mie ne trop chas ne trop frois.	Car when the limbs are large, there is no need to apply these plaisters sooner than after V days or VII, or more according to your safety from hot swelling; and here is the manner of old plasters for restoring breakage, dislocation, and twisting: and it is that you take flour that remains at the walls of the mill, and mix it with a large amount of egg, and apply this concoction, neither too clear nor too thick, and use it, and this is the common way of the plaster restoring breakage to many conditions, and indeed for women and small children, because it is not either too hot or too cold.
Autre maniere d’emplastre restorans la brisure et la dislocation et la torsion: pren З abareis et ladani et enule et asefetide et gomme arabique et mugat, ana .x. З.; mirre et aloes, ana .v. З.; alechil .viiij. З.; et en fai poudre, et les melle avec aubun d’uef; se li complexion de l’ome est chaude, si use de cest emplaustre, car il est de bone composition, et restoure les os brisiés hastanment, et vaut a plusors homes.	Another manner of poultice restoring the break and dislocation and twisting: take 3 albari (aloes) and ladanum and myrrh and gum arabic and myrrh root, each .x. drachms; frankincense and aloes, each .v. drachms; aloe 9 drachms; and make into powder, and mix with abundance of egg; if the complexion of the person is hot, use this poultice, because it is well composed, and restores broken bones quickly, and is worth many men.
Autre maniere d’emplaustre que vaut a torsion: pren mugat et vismalve, ana X З.; mirre et aloes, ana VIIII З.; acaice VI З.; bolic armenic X З., et broie bien, et confit avec aigue ou abun d’uef, et l’aministre.	Another manner of plaster that is worth for twisting: take myrrh and cloves, equal to ten drams; frankincense and aloes, equal to nine drams; aloe wood, six drams; Armenian balsam, ten drams, and grind well, and mix with vinegar or a large amount of egg, and administer.
Autre maniere d’emplaustre a jointes et as os desevrez de lor leus, et apaise la dolor sorvenant, et le taille usent de l’os, et la briseure: pren lainne novele, et la moille en aisil et en oile cuit, et met sus lou leu.	Another method of poultice for joints and bones that are dislocated from their place, and calms the pain ensuing; they use oil, cloth, and the breakage: take new linen, and steep it in ash and in cooked oil, and put it on the place.
Cist emplastres ne restoure mie, mais il est boins por apaisier d’aposteme chaut, et oste proprement les dolors.	These poultices do not restore, but they are good for soothing hot abscesses and properly remove pains.
Autre maniere d’emplaustre qui restoure les os brisiez: pren foilles de figuier seches, et foilles de pavol terrestre, et broie ensamble, et fai emplaustre.	Another manner of plaster that restores broken bones: take dry leaves of fig tree and earthy leaves, and crush them together, and make a plaster.
Autre maniere d’emplaustre a la restoration de la brisure de l’os et por amenusier lou remenant de l’aposteme.	Another method of poultice for the restoration of a bone fracture and to soften the remaining abscess.
Pren racine de vismalve, camomille, flors de violettes, farine de veces, de chescune une partie; et broie, et confis avec vin, et se li membres est eschaufez, se le confis avec aigue de coriandre moiste, ou d’aigue, et en use.	Take roots of balm, camomile, flowers of violets, flour of beans, each a part; and crush them, and mix with wine, and if the member is heated, mix with moist vinegar of coriander or vinegar, and use.
Autre maniere d’emplaustre plus fors que li premier, quant aposteme dur vient a la restauration dou membre.	Another manner of poultice more strong than the first, when a hard abscess comes to the restoration of the member.
Pren racines de vismalve, et semence de lin, balauste, mellilote, et sansuce, et flors de violetes et de camomille, de toutes ana une partie; et broie, et confit avec aigue de salz, ou aigue douce, ou avec vin, selonc la chalor du membre, et son repos.	Take roots of vervain, and seeds of flax, marshmallow, and licorice, and violets and chamomile flowers all in equal parts; and grind, and mix with salt water, or sweet water, or with wine, according to the heat of the member, and its rest.
Ypocras dist en son livre c’on ne mette sus lou membre brisiet por restorer fuer que cerote fait de cire et d’oile, et nulle autre chose plus, et dist qu’il soit entre dous ne trop molz ne trop durs.	Hippocrates says in his book that you should not put on the broken member to restore it anything more than wax made of beeswax and oil, and nothing else, and he says that it should be between too soft and too hard.
Et il semble galien c’on doit mettre sus lou membre brisiet, por restoreir, choses seches un poc chaudes, si com mirre et aloes et encens et semblans choses, si com nos avons dit.	And it seems to Galen that on the broken member, to restore it, one should put things that are dry and slightly warm, such as myrrh and aloe and incense and similar things, just as we have said.
Autre maniere d’emplaustre que vaut a lesion et a dolor: pren mugath et chevolz d’omes decopez, ou pennes d’oiseil, et vismalve, et seil igalment, et les broie, et en fai emplaustre et lou lie.	Another manner of plaster that is good for lesions and pain: take mugwort and chopped hazelnuts or feathers from a bird, and balm, all in equal parts, and crush them, and make a plaster with it and apply it to the area.
Et puis lou deslie un poc aprés, et pren garde se li malaides ne sent nule dolor, ne ne s’est meus li os de son leu; si ne lou deslie par mains jors Et se dolor vient au leu, ou prurite, sens repos ou enfleure, si te haste, et le deslie, et oste l’emplaustre maintenant; et pren drapeil legier, et esponge de mer moiste, et plonge en aigue, et en leve lou leu, tant que li prurite cesse, et soit apaisie li dolor; puis laisse reposer lou membre une houre, puis met sus lainne seche, moillie en aigue ou en oile roset, et lie sus une nut, tant que tu soies seur d’aposteme chaut; et repose li enflammation dou membre, et soit ostee li dolor, puis rencomence la lieure subtil, et un poc d’emplaustre, et ne l’estraindre mie si fort com devant; et subtilie tant qu’il soit sanez.	And then remove the poultice after a while, and be careful if the patient does not feel any pain, nor has the bone returned to its place; if you do not remove it by hand. And if there is pain at the site, or itching, without rest or swelling, hurry and remove it, and take off the poultice now; and take a light cloth, and a moist sea sponge, and plunge in water, and lift up the area, until the itching ceases, and the pain is appeased. Then let the member rest for an hour, then put on dry linen, dampened with water or rose oil, and tie it on a nut, as long as you are sure of hot abscess; and remove the inflammation from the member, and take away the pain, then begin the gentle tying again, and a bit of poultice, and do not strain it so hard as before; and tie gently until they are healed.
Et se tu vois que aposteme chaus et roujor et dolor et enfleure sont jai osteit dou tout, et tu as mestier d’emplaustre et de liien, si le rencomence, si com tu a fait premier igalment.	And if you see that the hot aposteme and redness and pain and swelling have been completely removed, and you need to apply plaster and bandage again, then reapply them, just as you did initially.
Et s’il ne vient au membre nient de ceu que nos avons dit, si ne le desliier fuer que aprés III jors ou IIII ou V ou VII; et aucune fois le laisse XX jors, selonc ce c’on voit, tant que li briseure s’aert, et aproche li ajostement de la char sus; adonques lou restraint plus que premier, et li engroisse sa viande, si com j’ai dit devant; et se tu vois lou leu sechier plus que mestier n’est, saiches que norrissemens n’i puet venir; arose lou donques d’aigue chaude tede, toutes les fois que tu lou deslies, et ce soit chescun tier jor, et eslaiche un poc la lieure; par cest ovraige donques cort li norrissemens au membre, et esmende hastanment ceu que font li fol mire, que restourent le membre tortement: c’est malvais, et si est ovraiges paourous, et je n’i voi autre chose que dou lassier et de l’ovrer droitement.	If it does not come to the member any of what we have said, then do not disjoin it until after three days or four or five or seven; and let it stay twenty days, as one sees, as long as the swelling subsides, and approaches the flesh above; at that point it is held more tightly than before, and its meat thickens, just as I said before. And if you see the place drying out more than necessary, know that swelling cannot come to it; then put hot water on it, every third day, and pour a spot of wine on it. By this work thus cuts off the swelling from the member, and quickly remedies what foolish practitioners have done, leaving the member twisted: that is bad, and it is fearful work, and I see no other thing than to let it alone and operate correctly.

Maintes manieres de brisures sont, et diverses figures, et les causes sont destintees; car il a brisures que sont faites de percussion d’espee, et li colz oste tout l’os jusc’a syfac qui est desoz l’os, si com fait haiche ou fust; et por ceu est apelee ceste maniere de briseure «haichure».	Many manners of breaks are, and diverse forms, and the causes are specified; for there are breaks that are caused by a blow from a sword, and the shoulder is broken so that all the bone goes as far as the hollow which is under the bone, just like what happens when it's hit by an axe or a club; and therefore this manner of break is called "haichure".
Ou li espee cope l’os par desus sens percier, et est apelee ceste maniere de brisure «absolue»; et est li plaie de ces .ij. brisures grans ou petite.	Or there are breaks where the sword strikes the bone above without piercing it, and is called this type of break "absolute"; and the wound from these two types of breaks is great or small.
Et s’il i a percussion emboee, ou fendure ou de colz de piere, ou de cheoir sus piere, ou d’autres semblans. Et s’il a brisure persant, qui aproiche jusc’a la toile qui est desoz l’os, ou elle est sus l’os, et est ceste plaie ou grans ou petite. Et si a plaie qui est reponue en l’os ausi subtil com uns chevous, et est li fendure petite, et por ceu est apelee ceste brisure «capillaire»; et si la plaie qui est faite ou par ferir de piere, ou par cheoir, ou par semblant chose, et fait entrer la piece de l’os aval, et est li leus chevez si com font li chaudelier quant il fierent sus les chaudieres; si entre li piere dedens.	And if there is a wound that has been closed in the bone just as finely as a net, and it is the crack small, and therefore this break is called "capillaire"; and if the wound is made either by being struck with an arrow, or by falling, or by something similar, and it causes the piece of bone to enter down inside, and it makes the place hollow just like a blacksmith does when they heat up the cauldron on top; then the arrow goes in.

And when the wound comes first to you, and it's in winter, then it is necessary to remove the bone before the 14th day of the moon (i.e. a lunar cycle), as soon as you can. The goal is to avoid damaging surrounding tissue or penetrating too deeply into the skull. The quality for piercing around the broken bone is that you place one forceps on the bone and turn it back and forth until you feel that the bone has been pierced; then move the forceps to another part a little later, and do this all around. de la brisure des costes	Il semble que nous sommes en train de discuter d'un texte médiéval sur la chirurgie, probablement le "Chirurgia" de Guy de Chauliac, un ouvrage de référence pour les chirurgiens du Moyen Âge.
Saiches que li briseure des costes ne vient fuer que en leus gros aprés le dos, et au chief dedens, et ne ploient fuer que por ceu qu’eles sont glairouses; et le sent om bien a l’inquisition as dois.	Know that the breaking of the ribs does not occur except in thick places after the back, and at the head inside, and they do not bend unless because they are greasy; and people know this well by inspection with their hands.
Et li restoremens si est que tu met igal la brisure, si com il covient.	And the remedy is that you put it back together evenly, as it should be.
Et puis met l’emplaustre, et l’estraint d’une estele, se tu en as mestier.	And then apply a poultice, and bind it with an iron band, if you need to.
Et se li brisure des costes est declinans au dedens, si sent li malaides tres grant dolor, et pointure, ausi com de pleuresin, car li os point la toile; et alaine a poinnes, et toust et vomist grant planteit de sanc, et c’est de grief santeit, et li ancien i ont mis grant engien.	And if the breaking of the ribs is declining inward, then the patient feels very great pain and puncture, as well as pleurisy, because the bone pierces the lung; and it swells with bruises, and burns and vomits a large quantity of blood, and that's a severe illness, and the ancient people have put great remedies.
Et si a de ces qui dient qu’il le covient aministrer viandes qui enflent le ventre, por bouter la briseure au defuer.	And there are those who say that it is necessary to administer foods that inflate the belly, in order to drive the break outward.
Nos ne faisons mie ceu, que apostemes chaulz n’i viegne, s’il n’i est venus.	We don't do this, so that hot abscesses don't form there, if they haven't already come.
Et s’il i est venus, si le saine.	And if it has come, let it heal.
Et dirent aucun d’ous c’on met ventouses sor le leu, por sucier forment, et c’est semblant a raison; toutevoie, doutance i a que li ventouse n’atraie les superfluitez au leu por la disposition de foiblece.	And they said among themselves that one puts cupping glasses on the place, to draw out the foul matter, and it seems reasonable; however, there is doubt that the cupping glass may attract the superfluous fluids to the place because of its weakness.
Et dirent aucun d’ous c’on doit covrir le leu de lainne moillie en oile chaut, et met on tentes entre les costes por metre igal la lieure entor, puis soit curez de la cure de pleurisin, de viande et de medicine.	And they said among themselves that one must cover the place with damp linen in hot oil, and put tents between the ribs to put equal pressure on the pleura all around, then it should be cured with the treatment of pleurisy, of meat and of medication.
Et se li malaides sent grant dolor qu’il ne puet soffrir par l’os qui point la toile, et doutance est au malaide, si covient fendre sus lou leu de la coste brisie, et covient mettre desoz I estrument por warder lou syfac; et adonques doit on trenchier l’os, et traire legierement, et puis coudre les II levres de la plaie, s’elle est grans, et le curez d’emplaustres, tant qu’il soit sanez.	And if the patient feels great pain that he cannot bear because of the bone which sticks into the cloth, and there is doubt in the patient, then it is necessary to cut on the place of the broken rib, and it is necessary to put a tool underneath to protect the diaphragm; and then one must saw the bone, and pull gently, and then sew up the two lips of the wound, if they are large, and heal them with poultices until he is sound.
Et se apostemes chaus vient, si met sus drapelz moilliez en oile roset, et met sus lou leu; et cure tandis le leu de ceu qu’il covient a aposteme, et soit gisans sovins sus lou costeit ou il doit, tant qu’il soit sanez.	And if an abscess comes, put damp cloths soaked in rose oil on it and apply to the place; and cure the place from which the abscess came, and be careful with bandages on its border where it should be until it is healed.
de la brisure dou col	of the breaking of the neck
Aucune fois vient briseure as os dou col, et poc avient k’il ploie; et ausi a l’espinee dou dos.	Never will there be a break in the bones of the neck, and it will not bend; and also in the spine of the back.
Et quant ce avient a aucun, et tu vues savoir s’il sera sanez ou non, si pren warde se tu vois ses mains mollifiies et enflees et mortes, et ne les puet movoir ne estendre, et quant tu les poins d’une aguille, si ne lou sent.	And when this happens to someone, and you want to know if they will be healed or not, take note if you see their hands softened and swollen and dead, and cannot move them or stretch them, and when you prick them with a needle, it does not feel.
Saiches qu’il est mortelz em plusors dispositions, et s’il les muet et sent bien quant on les estraint et point, saiches que li jointure de l’os est sauve, et que li malaides iert curez, s’on i met cure.	Know that it is mortal in many dispositions, and if they move and feel well when you restrain them and prick them, know that the joint of the bone is saved, and that the patient will be cured, if one puts a cure there.
Et se semblans chose vient a l’espinee dou dos, et tu vues savoir s’il iert sanez ou non, si pren warde se li piez sont amollis, et se li accident i sont qui sont em mains, et sus lou dos saut ventositeit et egestion sens volonteit; et quant on l’a sovineit sus son ventre, si saut egestion sens volonteit.	And if a similar thing comes to the spine of the back, and you want to know if it is healed or not, take note if the feet are softened, and if the accident that occurred are in the hands, and on the back there is ventosity and ejection without will; and when one has bound it on their belly, if there is ejection without will.
Et quant il est sovinez sus son dos et il vuet asseller, si ne puet.	And when he lies on his back and wants to sit up, he cannot.
Saiches qu’il est mortelz: si ne laboreir mie en sa cure.	Know that it is mortal: if not labored in its treatment.
Et se cist accident ne li vienent, si est li chose plus legiere.	And if this accident does not come to him, then the thing is less serious.
Et li cure si est que tu rewardes s’il i a aposteme chaut, si met sus l’espinee oile roset ou oile de moieuf d’uef, tant qu’il soit apaisiez; puis met sus un des emplaustres confortans et sechans, et le lie d’un liien, et comande au malaide a reposer; et ne dorme mie sus celle partie ou il sent la dolor, tant k’il soit sanez.	And the cure is that you rub it with oil of roses or oil of egg yolk if there is warm swelling, so that it calms down; then put on one of the comforting and drying plasters, and tie a bandage over it, and order the patient to rest; and do not let them sleep on the part where they feel pain, until they are cured.
Et se aucuns os se dessevre, si covient coper sus lou keur; et oste l’os, puis assemble les .ij. levres de la plaie, s’elle est grans, a une costure, puis le cure d’oignement enjanrant char, tant qu’il soit sanez; et se li chiés de l’os est brisiez, ki est apelez li «chiés de la kowe», se i boute ton pouchet de ta main senestre, et le met igal a l’autre main a ton pooir, puis met l’emplaustre et l’estele sus, se tu en a mestier; puis l’estraint, et s’il i a os desevreit, si fent sus, et l’oste, et cure la plaie tant qu’elle soit sanee.	And if some bone is broken, it's necessary to cover it with a splint; and take out the bone, then join together the two edges of the wound, if it's large, with a suture; then treat it with ointment dripping fat, until they are cured. And if the ligament of the bone is torn, which is called the "ligament of the joint", take your left hand and put it in a pouch, and place it equally to the other hand at your power, then put the plaster and the splint on, if you need them; then restrain it, and if there are loose bones, do up the splint, and remove the bone, and treat the wound until it is healed.
de la brisure de l’os de la hainche	It seems like we've moved on to a specific medical condition. "De la brisure de l'os de la haniche" is an Old French phrase that refers to the fracture of the hip bone.
Poc avient que li os de la hainche soit brisiez, et s’il avient, si n’avient fuer c’un poc d’amenuisement au chief; et fent dou lonc, et decline au pardedens, et vient au malaide dolor au leu, et pointure, et enfle ceu que brise.	Here is the translation of the text line-by-line:
Et li restoration si est que tu estendes ta main sus, tant que tu soies sus la briseure; et saiches quelz est li brisure et li figure de li; et se li brisure est au chief soulement, si met igal cele brisure, tant qu’ele soit a figure natureil.	And the restoration is that you stretch your hand up over so that you are above the fracture; and know what the fracture is and its shape; and if the fracture is only in the head, put an equal piece of it back, so that it is in natural form.
Et se li brisure est dou lonc, ou elle decline au dedens, si fai gesir le malaide sus son ventre, tant que li restauration de cele brisure te soit aparillie.	And if the fracture is too long, or it declines inside, then make the patient lie on his stomach, so that the restoration of this fracture will be visible to you.
Et quant tu l’avras mise igal, si met sus l’emplaustre, puis met sus une estele de fust ou de keur, et estraint la briseure, si qu’ele ne se mueve; et emple le leu desoz les costes, tant que li lieure soit igalz.	And when you have put it back in place, then put on a splint, next put on a wooden or horn splint, and bind the fracture so that it does not move; and apply an ointment under the ribs, until the joint is equal.
Et comande au malaide k’il dorme sus son costeit sain, ou sus son dos.	And command the patient to sleep on his healthy side, or on his back.

And if swelling comes to it, do not touch it, until the swelling is calmed, and put on what we have said before; and then return to the restoration, and bind it as it needs, if there is no bad accident; and that is the way.
de la brisure de l’ajutoire	of the arm fracture
Li adjutoires est ceu qui est entre lou coute et lou chief de l’espaule, et s’il est brisiez, si le doit on restorer selonc l’une des .ij. manieres: dont l’une est que tu pren .i. fust plain de moiene groissor, selonc ceste forme:	The arm is that which is between the elbow and the shoulder head, and if it is broken, then it must be restored in one of two ways: one being to take one piece of wood, smoother on one side larger.
Et lie as .ij. chiés .ij. liens, et les pent en haut, et siece li malaide sus .i. siege; puis li giete som bras brisiet sus le fust, tant qu’il viegne a l’aissele, et ait em mi une corbeure de fust, puis pent sus aucune pesant chose ou li ministres l’estende aval; puis mette li mires la briseure igal a ses mains ensamble, tant que li briseure retorne si com il covient.	And tie the two ends of the wood with two strings, and bend them up, and make the patient sit on one seat; then throw the broken arm on the stick, so that it comes to rest, and has in the middle a curve of wood; then place something heavy on it or have someone extend it downwards; then put the splint alongside the break, so that the break returns to its proper shape.
Et li autre maniere si est que on sovine le malaide sus son hatereil, et li lie la main a son col, puis comande a .ij. ministres que li uns teigne ceu qui est sus la briseure a ses mains, et li autres teigne desoz a tout .i. liien, et estende chescuns a sa partie.	And the other way is that you have the patient lie on his back, and tie his hand to his neck; then command two ministers to take one end of the splint and tie it to his hands, and the other minister to tie the other end under each strap, and stretch both ends to their proper place.
Et se tu vues que li estraction soit plus fors, si estraint I liien desoz la briseure, et sus la briseure, et estende chescuns des ministres a sa part.	And if you see that the extraction is more difficult, then hold the thread under the breakage, and on top of the breakage, and stretch each of the ligaments to its part.
Et se li briseure est prochiene au chief de l’espaule, si covient que tu mettes I liien moien desoz l’assele, et I autre vers lou coute, desoz la briseure; et ausi se li briseure est devers lou coute, si i covient mettre I liien legierement, sens labor, tant que li briseure soit igalz sus lou leu, et sus lou coute; puis met igal la brisure, si com il covient, et l’estraint, se apostemes chaus n’i vient.	And if the breakage is near the head of the shoulder, it is necessary that you put the middle thread under the bone, and the other towards the armpit, under the breakage; and also if the breakage is towards the armpit, it is necessary to put the lighter thread, without labor, until the breakage be even on the bone and on the armpit; then make equal the breakage, as it is necessary, and hold it, lest abscesses come there.
Et se apostemes chaus i vient, si ne l’estraindre mie jusc’a .vij. jors, et met sus lainne moillie en aisil et en oile roset, tant que li apostemes chaus soit apaisiez; puis l’estraint, et li maniere de l’estraindre si est que tu mettes l’emplaustre sus la briseure, puis assemble le bras sus l’ajutoire, et met ta main sus l’espaule ou sus lou sein; et met le drapeil, et estraint sus l’ajutoire, et sus lou bras, si que li bras soit en leu d’esteles, s’aucune chose ne le te deffent, ou se li os n’est muez.	And if swelling comes, do not bind it again until 7 days have passed, and put a wet compress on the arm and rose oil, until the swelling is calmed; then bind it, and the way to bind it is that you put an emplastron (a type of medicinal plaster) on the break, then bring the arm onto the armpit, and place your hand on the shoulder or breast, and put a bandage around the arm and armpit, so that the arm is in its proper position, if nothing prevents it, or if the bone is not moved.
Et se tu doutes que li os ne se mueve, si use d’esteles, et met sus la brisure une estele plus large et plus fort des autres, et ait entre chescune estele .i. doi d’espaice, et soit chescune estele si grans qu’elle passe la briseure .iij. dois de chescune part; puis estrain sus les esteles, ensi com j’ai dit au comencement dou chapistre sus la briseure, plus fort et moins en aloignant.	And if you doubt that the bone is not in its proper place, use a splint and put on the break a bandage that is wider and stronger than the others, with two spaces between each one, and so that each bandage passes over the break three fingers from either side; then bind it on the splints, as I said at the beginning of this chapter, tighter and less spaced.
Et se tu vois l’assise des esteles, et l’estrignement, si com nos avons dit, en l’oure que tu restoures le membre brisiet, et tu doutes l’aposteme chaut, si le laisse a liier jusc’a septime jor, si com nos avons dit; puis regarde chescuns III jors lou lien, que prurite ne enfleure n’i viegne au leu, ou li norrissemens ne puist venir au membre por ceu k’il est trop estrois.	And if you see the sitting of stars, and the constriction, as we have said, in the operation that you restore the broken member, and you doubt the hot abscess, so leave it to attach itself until the seventh day, as we have said; then look every three days at the ligature, lest itching or swelling comes to the place, or the discoloration cannot come to the member because it is too narrow.
Si l’adrece, si com nos avons dit.	If the redness, as we have said.
Et se nulz telz accidens n’i vient, si ne lou desliier fuer que aprés III jors ou plus, et dorme li malaides sus son dos, et ait ses mains sus son stomac; et met desoz l’ajutoire I cussin plain de laine igalment, et regarde chescune houre dou jor ou de la nut, que li figure dou membre brisiet ne se mueve, ou ne soit amollis li liiens; si l’adrece par la vertu de medicine, et li done la viande que nos avons devant mis, en teil maniere que li viande soit au premier subtilz; et quant il comance a reprendre, si soit grosse.	And if no such accident comes to it, then do not remove the ligature until after three days or more, and let the patient sleep on their back, and have their hands on their stomach; and place under the aid one pillow full of wool evenly, and look every hour of day or night that the figure of the broken member does not move, or that the tendons become soft; if the redness comes by the virtue of medicine, and give it food as we have previously mentioned, in such a manner that the food is at its first subtlety; and when he begins to recover, let it be substantial.
